  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1750040962" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12364301797863483445">The start of this book feels like McEwan in elder statesman mode, sitting down at his laptop, rolling up his sleeves and saying, ‘Right, out the way, fuckwads, let me show you how it's done.’ It's so conspicuously brilliant, so controlled and aware and unusual, that although the rest of the book can't quite sustain the ferocity of the first fifty pages, still this rarely felt like it was going to be be getting less than full marks from me.<br/><br/><i>Nutshell</i> is a sly contemporary version of <i>Hamlet</i>, where t</span>
  <span id="freeText12364301797863483445" style="display:none">The start of this book feels like McEwan in elder statesman mode, sitting down at his laptop, rolling up his sleeves and saying, ‘Right, out the way, fuckwads, let me show you how it's done.’ It's so conspicuously brilliant, so controlled and aware and unusual, that although the rest of the book can't quite sustain the ferocity of the first fifty pages, still this rarely felt like it was going to be be getting less than full marks from me.<br /><br /><i>Nutshell</i> is a sly contemporary version of <i>Hamlet</i>, where the infidelity between ‘Trudy’ and her brother-in-law, ‘Claude’, is observed not by a teenage prince but by a nine-month-old foetus two weeks away from his due date. This may seem like a youngish choice, as narrators go, but McEwan's prepartum protagonist is a joy of a companion – witty, eloquent, someone who enjoys a nice glass or two of Sancerre ‘decanted through a healthy placenta’ and who has plenty of time and material for contemplative flights of fancy: ‘my thoughts as well as my head,’ he notes, ‘are fully engaged.’<br /><br /><blockquote>I'm immersed in abstractions, and only the proliferating relations between them create the illusion of a known world. When I hear ‘blue’, which I've never seen, I imagine some kind of mental event that's fairly close to ‘green’ – which I've never seen. I count myself an innocent, unburdened by allegiances and obligations, a free spirit, despite my meagre living room. No one to contradict or reprimand me, no name or previous address, no religion, no debts, no enemies. My appointment diary, if it existed, notes only my forthcoming birthday. I am, or I was, despite what the geneticists are now saying, a blank slate.</blockquote><br /><br />The phrase-making is lovely on every page of this book; nothing jars, no word is out of place, no odd juxtapositions leave you wishing the manuscript had been scanned by an editor one final time. ‘In heavy rains the drains, like dependable banks, return their deposit with interest’ – this is the sort of thing he tosses off in the middle of a paragraph – or dubbing the repeated exclamation <i>unless</i> ‘a bleating little iamb of hope’. We are invited to look forward to ‘the dim-lit gore of the delivery room’, and induced to think of the unborn as ‘po-faced stoics, submerged Buddhas, expressionless’. One finds oneself finishing a page and then skipping back to the top, ostensibly to double-check some plot-point, but really just to enjoy it all a second time.<br /><br />But the main pleasure is in seeing how well McEwan makes use of his gimmick to come at every scene from a new angle.<br /><br /><blockquote>When I hear the friendly drone of passing cars and a slight breeze stirs what I believe are horse chestnut leaves, when a portable radio below me tinnily rasps and a penumbral coral glow, a prolonged tropical dusk, dully illuminates my inland sea and its trillion drifting fragments, then I know that my mother is sunbathing on the balcony outside my father's library.</blockquote><br /><br />The new perspective it allows us on sex scenes is fully and alarmingly explored, in a series of encounters that are by turns hilarious, moving and horrific:<br /><br /><blockquote>Not everyone knows what it is to have your father's rival's penis inches from your nose. By this late stage they should be refraining on my behalf. Courtesy, if not clinical judgement, demands it. I close my eyes, I grit my gums, I brace myself against the uterine walls. […] On each occasion, on every piston stroke, I dread that he'll break through and shaft my soft-boned skull and seed my thoughts with his essence, with the teeming cream of his banality. Then, brain-damaged, I'll think and speak like him. I'll be the son of Claude.</blockquote><br /><br />I mean I could go on just quoting paragraphs I thought were remarkable, but these are so numerous that the review would start to be conterminous with its subject. Suffice to say this is a book to be enjoyed on a sentence-by-sentence level – and along the way McEwan finds time to reflect on climate change, religious extremism, social justice warriors and identity politics, not to mention throwing in a load of cheekily unmarked Shakespearean quotes, some of which I caught and many of which I probably missed.<br /><br />Overall, and contrary to my vague memories of McEwan (a writer I don't know well), the general tone here is cautiously upbeat. Although the story being told is, famously, a tragic one, still the message that emerges from the text is that telling stories about tragedies is our best hope of dealing with them. After all, beyond an occasional kick, there's nothing our narrator can do to act on or influence events; what he can do is think about them, analyse them, talk about them, play with words and ideas and concepts. Perhaps it's through this process that tragedy can become something transcendent – a suitable subject for world literature. As our embryonic hero puts it, ‘God said, Let there be pain. And there was poetry. Eventually.’</span>
  <a data-text-id="12364301797863483445" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1734396230" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1734396230" />
    <a title="Debbie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8665845-debbie"><img alt="Debbie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1334166735p2/8665845.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1734396230?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Debbie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Debbie" href="/user/show/8665845-debbie">Debbie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=blew-my-mind">blew-my-mind</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=favorite-thrillers">favorite-thrillers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=cultural">cultural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=mostly-not-set-in-u-s">mostly-not-set-in-u-s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=imagination-rules">imagination-rules</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=best-reads-2012-through-2019">best-reads-2012-through-2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=favorite-authors">favorite-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1734396230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer650514097744276054">OMG OMG OMG! This book really did knock my socks off. In fact, it goes on my all-time favorites list. Who could resist this bizarro opening line?<br/><br/><i>So here I am, upside down in a woman. Arms patiently crossed, waiting, waiting and wondering who I’m in, what I’m in for.</i><br/><br/>Okay, so there are some very good writers who can give you very good characters and very good metaphors and a very good plot with very good prose.<br/><br/>And then there's Ian McEwan. A genius. This guy, he's in a league of his own. His word p</span>
  <span id="freeText650514097744276054" style="display:none">OMG OMG OMG! This book really did knock my socks off. In fact, it goes on my all-time favorites list. Who could resist this bizarro opening line?<br /><br /><i>So here I am, upside down in a woman. Arms patiently crossed, waiting, waiting and wondering who I’m in, what I’m in for.</i><br /><br />Okay, so there are some very good writers who can give you very good characters and very good metaphors and a very good plot with very good prose.<br /><br />And then there's Ian McEwan. A genius. This guy, he's in a league of his own. His word play, his creativity, his sophistication, his insights, his descriptions of the little unnoticed things that snap me to attention. His language is artful and funny, incredibly cool and dance-y. He’s an amazing storyteller.<br /><br />This dark and edgy story told by a soon-to-be-born fetus is so clever and bizarre, I just dove right in, trusting that McEwan would give me a trip to remember, and that he did. Please don’t cringe—the fact that the story is told by a fetus is not to be taken seriously. Mostly, guys, it’s just an incredibly brilliant way to get an original and fascinating omniscient author into the story. Our little fetus is a first-class eavesdropper. And believe me, he is nothing like a fetus—he’s more like an erudite, sometimes grumpy, old man with a killer vocabulary. He’s wise beyond his non-years, and is, of course, concerned about his safety (his mother is a total lush). He’s also endearing, brilliant, super observant, curious, cynical, and very funny. This isn’t fantasy, I promise—it’s absurdist fiction at its best. Think <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/485894.The_Metamorphosis" title="The Metamorphosis by Franz Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">The Metamorphosis</a> by Kafka.<br /><br />What’s happening outside the womb is a scheme that his mother and her lover cook up, both of whom are super unlikeable. The narrator has a field day describing the crime, and there’s suspense as we wait to see if they get caught. The pacing is good.<br /><br />That said, this was a slow read, and that’s because I often wanted to reread sentences. Sometimes I reread because I just wanted to--every sentence is jazz, so I played the record a couple of times, loving the tone and the rhythm. Sometimes I reread because I needed to concentrate more to comprehend the full glory. There were lots of new words for me, and of course I had to check out definitions. It is a short book, so for fast readers with less pit stops it should be a quick read. I want to say this is an intellectual book, which usually is not a plus for me. Here, I was mesmerized. McEwan manages to be philosophical without being pedantic or lecture-y.<br /><br />There were maybe three times when McEwan did pontificate a bit (there were a few pages on the structure of poetry, for instance), but I was so in love with his prose and his story I didn’t give a damn. And oh my god, what a lot of highlighting I did.<br /><br />Here’s some cool philosophy, McEwan showing his serious side:<br /><br /><i>Pessimism is too easy, even delicious, the badge and plume of intellectuals everywhere. It absolves the thinking classes of solutions.</i><br /><br />And here are a couple womb-related sentences that cracked me up:<br /><br /><i>Anxiously, I finger my cord. It serves for worry beads.</i><br /><br /><i>Not everyone knows what it is like to have your father’s rival’s penis inches from your nose.</i><br /> <br />I want jazz, I want percussion, I want language that plays wildly in my head as well as my heart. I want to be wide-eyed and hanging on every word. This book delivered. I now have to read all of his books, end of story.<br /><br />Well . . . almost. When, nice and drowsy for once, I closed this book, I saw a fucking spider walking across my sheet. A spider! In my BED! Oh god!!! Something in my advanced age I had luckily never ever seen before. And it wasn’t tiny—it was the size of a dime. This woke me the fuck up! Shivering with fear, I leapt from my bed, got some toilet paper, and squished it to death. And no, I wasn’t proud of my killer instincts but I was on automatic. Spider in bed, must die! Then I thought of all the terrifying what-ifs. What if it had already bitten me (god, had it actually been strolling across my SKIN!!!??) and I would soon be in severe pain or itch-land? What if it’s a momma who just delivered babies near my toes and she was just going to search for chow, some mite bites. (Which means, oh the poor babies, I’ve murdered their mother. But worse, do I have a spider family sharing my bed and about to creepy-crawl all over me?) What if her man is resting in my covers, and forlorn and suspicious when wife-y-poo doesn’t return, he decides to attack? Then into my mind popped this question: what if the spider wants to talk about his arduous trek and the awful giant who squished him? Or what if the poor abandoned baby spider needs to tell her traumatic story? So just maybe it’s time for a spider narrator. . . .</span>
  <a data-text-id="650514097744276054" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="comment_211816914" class="brown_comment">
  <div class="xhr_comment_photo">
    	<a alt="Pauline" href="/user/show/59696537-pauline"><img src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_25x33-ccd24e68f4773d33a41ce08c3a34892e.png" alt="U 25x33" /></a>

  <div class="xhr_comment_body">
    <a style="font-weight:bold" href="/user/show/59696537-pauline">Pauline</a>

      
<span id="freeTextContainer7770536046171785753">I love your review! Yes, McEwan is in a category of his own. His novels are always a healthy mix between being provocative, intellectually challenging</span>
  <span id="freeText7770536046171785753" style="display:none">I love your review! Yes, McEwan is in a category of his own. His novels are always a healthy mix between being provocative, intellectually challenging and down to earth. <br /><br />I can’t help but noticing some patterns in his works which make them stand out. Unlike most of contemporary writers, for one thing, sex is neither absent nor matter of factly depicted for itself. Sex, in McEwan’s books, is pure violence, animality, and it carries a deep meaning in each scene where it is depicted with a specific purpose in the storyline. This is no exception here. Trudy, Claude, mice, spiders, Elodie, everything in that house is related to animals at some point. The sex scenes, the dirty house, the questioning about the origins, the intellectual podcasts are responding to each other in a brilliant way, portraying a world of thinking animals, ready for violence and murder any time, but also able of deep thinking, profound soul questioning.<br /><br />Another thing that distinguishes McEwan is the way he uses irony for little, apparently unimportant details. There are some pages where I have to turn several times the words in my head to get the whole meaning of what is implied. Not that it is complicated, but it has multiple meanings. With McEwan, I am not only reading, I am forced to be involved in the book by having to think actively. Using words which have only one signification to express several layers of ideas, that’s literature. I will probably never stop reading his books, as long as I can cope with only one novel a year from him.<br /><br />This being said, this one disappointed me a bit. It might have to do with level of expectations I had due to the writer, but also to how brilliant this idea of writing about being in the womb from the standpoint of the unborn child is. I can’t stress out too much how innovating, creative, profound this idea is. And the novel starts exactly as I thought it would, questioning pretty much any of the set of ideas and prejudices we have towards pregnancy, returning a cliché into a beautiful, deep human quest for understanding of how matter can be. However, I felt at some point that this potential got overturned at the benefit of the murder in itself, which had a surprisingly simple storyline. Usually, McEwan surprises his readers by a twist that shakes the whole trajectory of the novel in a new direction. Here, somehow, the trajectory remains exactly the same as expected. I feel like I have just read a wonderful novel by a wonderful writer, which could - should I write “should”- have been out of this world. So...expectations too high I guess.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7770536046171785753" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <a title="Diane S ☔" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4159922-diane-s"><img alt="Diane S ☔" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598402138p2/4159922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1714038196?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane S ☔" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane S ☔" href="/user/show/4159922-diane-s">Diane S ☔</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781911214335" href="/book/show/29752912-nutshell">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1714038196" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2296255684687775178">A book that is narrated by an eight month old fetus. He describes what he see and hears, from his father and his love of poetry to the nefarious plans of his mother and his uncle, his father's own brother. <br/><br/>So why did I have such a disconnect with this book? The writing is wonderful, amazing in places. Was it that I had a hard time envisioning a fetus using this level of thought and speech? Not sure, though I did find myself occasionally shaking my head at the thought especially since I am not a </span>
  <span id="freeText2296255684687775178" style="display:none">A book that is narrated by an eight month old fetus. He describes what he see and hears, from his father and his love of poetry to the nefarious plans of his mother and his uncle, his father's own brother. <br /><br />So why did I have such a disconnect with this book? The writing is wonderful, amazing in places. Was it that I had a hard time envisioning a fetus using this level of thought and speech? Not sure, though I did find myself occasionally shaking my head at the thought especially since I am not a fantasy lover, maybe I had a hard time going there?. I do admire the originality of the author's vision though. I think what frustrated me the most was all the thought side trips, just a little too much going astray here and there. This is definitely one intelligent fetus. Sometimes though, less is more and that is really how felt. It was just too much. Still I was glad of the experience of reading this very original story and ingesting this author's amazing prose.<br /><br />ARC from Netgalley.</span>

<div id="review_1752359873" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1752359873" />
    <a title="Adina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22189348-adina"><img alt="Adina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1531917330p2/22189348.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1752359873?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adina" href="/user/show/22189348-adina">Adina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781911214335" href="/book/show/29752912-nutshell">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22189348-adina?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22189348-adina?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22189348-adina?shelf=mcewan-barnes-coetzee-and-co">mcewan-barnes-coetzee-and-co</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22189348-adina?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1752359873" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer276777491112859522">Update 2: I found another interview with the author in a podcast. I had no idea McEwan is so funny. People at work were probably wondering why I was giggling while listening to this. Recommended!(it starts at minute 26 after the interview with Margaret Atwood). <br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.penguin.co.uk/articles/in-conversation/podcasts/vintage-podcast/2016/margaret-atwood-ian-mcewan/'>https://www.penguin.co.uk/articles/in...</a><br/><br/>Update: I found an interview of the author regarding the novel, how he got the idea to write it and where it stands in relation to his other works. <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.facebook.com/vintagebooks/videos/10154399398004774/'>https://www.facebook.com/vintagebooks...</a><br/><br/>*<br/>McEwan wr</span>
  <span id="freeText276777491112859522" style="display:none">Update 2: I found another interview with the author in a podcast. I had no idea McEwan is so funny. People at work were probably wondering why I was giggling while listening to this. Recommended!(it starts at minute 26 after the interview with Margaret Atwood). <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.penguin.co.uk/articles/in-conversation/podcasts/vintage-podcast/2016/margaret-atwood-ian-mcewan/">https://www.penguin.co.uk/articles/in...</a><br /><br />Update: I found an interview of the author regarding the novel, how he got the idea to write it and where it stands in relation to his other works. <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/vintagebooks/videos/10154399398004774/">https://www.facebook.com/vintagebooks...</a><br /><br />*<br />McEwan writes one of his best novels as an original and loose retelling of Hamlet, just in time for the commemoration of 400 years anniversary from Shakespeare’s death. The unique vision comes from the narrator, a fetus in the last few weeks of his in utero life stage. <br /><br />Trudy (Gertrude) and Claude (Claudius) have murder on their minds. Trudy betrays her husband, John, with, you guessed it, his brother Claude. A poet and an owner of small publishing house which not going very well; John has inherited an old but valuable mansion which is enough to spark the murderous imagination of the two lovers. The unwilling witness to the wicked schemes is the unborn baby of Trudy and John, the unnamed Hamlet. From his mother’s womb, he hears and feels everything. <br /><br />This is not strictly a crime novel. Although it has as main plot a villainous plan, the joy of reading this novel come from the witty, contemplative, funny reflections of the in utero narrator. Though some might be skeptical of the fetus omniscient level of thought and speech I thought it worked very well here. Fueled by the radio podcasts played by his mother, the narrator launches himself in various meditations on the world affairs, religious extremism, climate change, armed conflicts and other current subjects. <br /><br /><i>"Psychopaths are a constant fraction, a human constant. Armed struggle, just or not, attracts them. They help to tip local struggles into bigger conflicts. Europe, (…) in existential crisis, fractious and weak as varieties of self-loving nationalism sip that same tasty brew. Confusion about values, the bacillus of Anti- Semitism incubating, immigrant populations languishing, angry and bored. "</i><br /><br /><i>"Free speech no longer free, liberal democracy no longer the obvious port of destiny, robots stealing jobs, liberty in close combat with security, socialism in disgrace, capitalism corrupt, destructive and in disgrace, no alternatives in sight." </i><br /><br />It has to be said that the narrator is a bit of a snob and some of his opinions are not entirely PC and his opinions of British middle class might be harder to stomach by some. <br /><br />When he is not discussing world affairs he is contemplating his state in the womb, the feelings for his creators and his poor fate when he will have to face the world outside. <br /><br />Our fetus is also a wine connoisseur. During his sojourn in the womb he enjoys quite a few glasses of wine. Instead of being appalled by the idea that a fetus is intoxicated by his irresponsible mother, the incredible skill of McEwan made me sympathize with the child’s joyous drunkenness. I might have also poured myself a glass of Marlborough when it was mentioned somewhere in the novel. It might not be the narrator’s favorite but it is mine. <br /><br /><i>"I like to share a glass with my mother. You may never have experienced, or you will have forgotten, a good burgundy (her favourite) or a good Sancerre (also her favourite) decanted through a healthy placenta. Even before the wine arrives—tonight, a Jean-Max Roger Sancerre—at the sound of a drawn cork, I feel it on my face like the caress of a summer breeze."</i><br /><br />The unique condition of the narrator allows for some disturbing sex scenes, witnessed from a perspective that is not very often encountered in literature. <br /><br /><i>"Not everyone knows what it is to have your father's rival's penis inches from your nose. By this late stage they should be refraining on my behalf. Courtesy, if not clinical judgement, demands it. I close my eyes, I grit my gums, I brace myself against the uterine walls. […] On each occasion, on every piston stroke, I dread that he'll break through and shaft my soft-boned skull and seed my thoughts with his essence, with the teeming cream of his banality. Then, brain-damaged, I'll think and speak like him. I'll be the son of Claude."</i><br /><br />Ian McEwan is in this novel at the highest level of his world play. It should be enjoyed sentence by sentence, word by word. I found myself many times re-reading the sentences in order to fully absorb their brilliance. <br /><br />I could go on forever praising this novel and quoting from it but I will stop now. Suffice to say that it is wonderful, a great read for any of McEwan’s fans and a good starting point for those who never tried his work. Do not be put off by the narrator’s perspective, you do not need to be a fantasy reader to enjoy this. <br /><br /><i>Many thanks to Ian McEwan, Random House/Vintage, and Netgalley for this copy in exchange for an honest review.</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="276777491112859522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1753781099" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1753781099" />
    <a title="Trin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/113980-trin"><img alt="Trin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1181029461p2/113980.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1753781099?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trin" href="/user/show/113980-trin">Trin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/113980-trin?shelf=2010s">2010s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/113980-trin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/113980-trin?shelf=english-lit">english-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/113980-trin?shelf=ficbutnot">ficbutnot</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1753781099" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3676095105332797756">A modern retelling of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1420.Hamlet' title='Hamlet by William Shakespeare' rel='nofollow noopener'>Hamlet</a>, narrated by the infant prince from inside his mother's womb. It is every bit as insufferable as that sounds.<br/><br/>Ian McEwan is one of those writers who, having been crowned an author of <i>literature</i>, thinks he can write any piece of cracked-out nonsense and know it will be treated as a serious work. Is he taking the piss? Who knows. What I do know is: this book is a joke. I've liked other works of McEwan's, although even my favorite, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13562049.Sweet_Tooth' title='Sweet Tooth by Ian McEwan' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sweet Tooth</a>, contained elements that we</span>
  <span id="freeText3676095105332797756" style="display:none">A modern retelling of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1420.Hamlet" title="Hamlet by William Shakespeare" rel="nofollow noopener">Hamlet</a>, narrated by the infant prince from inside his mother's womb. It is every bit as insufferable as that sounds.<br /><br />Ian McEwan is one of those writers who, having been crowned an author of <i>literature</i>, thinks he can write any piece of cracked-out nonsense and know it will be treated as a serious work. Is he taking the piss? Who knows. What I do know is: this book is a joke. I've liked other works of McEwan's, although even my favorite, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13562049.Sweet_Tooth" title="Sweet Tooth by Ian McEwan" rel="nofollow noopener">Sweet Tooth</a>, contained elements that were highly problematic -- gotta love that nasty streak of British misogyny! But really he just writes hammy melodrama, often punctuated by a "twist," and dresses them up with pretentious prose. At his worst -- which this is -- he is absolutely the M. Night Shyamalan of authors.<br /><br />McEwan's main conceit -- the narrating from the womb thing -- is pretty stupid, but not entirely unworkable. Here's the first paragraph to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10015.Kate_Atkinson" title="Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Kate Atkinson</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28940.Behind_the_Scenes_at_the_Museum" title="Behind the Scenes at the Museum by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Behind the Scenes at the Museum</a>:<br /><br /><blockquote>I exist! I am conceived to the chimes of midnight on the clock on the mantelpiece in the room across the hall. The clock once belonged to my great-grandmother (a woman called Alice) and its tired chime counts me into the world. I'm begun on the first stroke and finished on the last when my father rolls off my mother and is plunged into a dreamless sleep, thanks to the five pints of John Smith's Best Bitter he has drunk in the Punch Bowl with his friends, Walter and Bernard Belling. At the moment at which I moved from nothingness into being my mother was pretending to be asleep -- as she often does at such moments. My father, however, is made of stern stuff and he didn't let that put him off.</blockquote><br /><br />Energy! Verve! Humor!<br /><br />In contrast, here's the opening paragraph to <i>Nutshell</i>:<br /><br /><blockquote>So here I am, upside down in a woman. Arms patiently crossed, waiting, waiting and wondering who I'm in, what I'm in for. My eyes close nostalgically when I remember how I once drifted in my translucent body bag, floated dreamily in the bubble of my thoughts through my private ocean in slow-motion somersaults, colliding gently against the transparent bounds of my confinement, the confiding membrane that vibrated with, even as it muffled, the voices of conspirators in a vile enterprise. That was in my careless youth. Now, fully inverted, not an inch of space to myself, knees crammed against belly, my thoughts as well as my head are fully engaged. I've no choice, my ear is pressed all day and night against bloody walls. I listen, make mental notes, and I'm troubled. I'm hearing pillow talk of deadly intent and I'm terrified by what awaits me, by what might draw me in.</blockquote><br /><br />Oh my god. Where's Laertes to put him out of his misery already?<br /><br />There are only 197 pages of this solipsistic shit, but it feels like a thousand. I'll admit it: I knew I would loathe this book by the time I had finished the above paragraph, but I hate-read it all the way to the end. I wanted to be thorough and complete in my disdain. But I can save you the trouble. In a nutshell: what a piece of crap.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3676095105332797756" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1954206613" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1954206613" />
    <a title="Hannah Greendale" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43377498-hannah-greendale"><img alt="Hannah Greendale" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466436186p2/43377498.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1954206613?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hannah Greendale" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hannah Greendale" href="/user/show/43377498-hannah-greendale">Hannah Greendale</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781524734091" href="/book/show/30016145-nutshell">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43377498-hannah-greendale?shelf=literary">literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43377498-hannah-greendale?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1954206613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17684957881021016398"><a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=pq6yNEcBbVo' rel='nofollow noopener'>Click here</a> to watch a video review of this book on my channel, <i>From Beginning to Bookend</i>.<br/><br/>A nine-month-old baby boy resides in the womb of Trudy. Unaware that her baby is listening and is witness to all she does, Trudy concocts a sinister scheme with a mysterious cohort, a deceitful plan of betrayal and, possibly, murder. <br/><br/><i>Nutshell</i> brings one of the most unique perspectives to storytelling. The limitations of writing an entire narrative from the viewpoint of a baby in the womb is not without its c</span>
  <span id="freeText17684957881021016398" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=pq6yNEcBbVo" rel="nofollow noopener">Click here</a> to watch a video review of this book on my channel, <i>From Beginning to Bookend</i>.<br /><br />A nine-month-old baby boy resides in the womb of Trudy. Unaware that her baby is listening and is witness to all she does, Trudy concocts a sinister scheme with a mysterious cohort, a deceitful plan of betrayal and, possibly, murder. <br /><br /><i>Nutshell</i> brings one of the most unique perspectives to storytelling. The limitations of writing an entire narrative from the viewpoint of a baby in the womb is not without its challenges, but the author succeeds in overcoming the hurdles that coincide with his use of such an unusual narrator. <br /><br /><i>How is it that I, not even young, not even born yesterday, could know so much, or know enough to be wrong about so much? I have my sources, I</i> listen<i>. My mother, Trudy, when she isn't with her friend Claude, likes the radio and prefers talk to music.</i> <br /><br />Despite the explanation for how the baby learns while in the womb, he is an exceptionally articulate narrator with a vocabulary that belies his age a tad too far. This is somewhat easy to forgive, because the writing is spectacular, but it's nonetheless noticeable that a baby who admits to having no understanding of what colors look like could so accurately describe furniture, or the ocean, or any number of other items he's never seen. <br /><br /><i>Pickled herring, gherkin, a slice of lemon on pumpernickel bread. They don't take long to reach me. Soon I'm whipped into alertness by a keen essence saltier than blood, by the tang of sea spray off the wide, open ocean road where lonely herring shoals skim northwards through clean black icy water. It keeps coming, a chilling Arctic breeze pouring over my face, as though I stood boldly in the prow of a fearless ship heading into glacial freedom.</i> <br /><br />Occasionally the baby partakes of philosophical thinking or offers social commentary that is mercilessly accurate. <br /><br /><i> And foe-of-convenience, the United States, barely the hope of the world, guilty of torture, helpless before its sacred text conceived in an age of powdered wigs, a constitution as unchallengeable as the Koran. Its nervous population obese, fearful, tormented by inarticulate anger, contemptuous of governance, murdering sleep with every new handgun.</i> <br /><br />But it's the baby's connection to his devious mother that fascinates. His understanding of her body, the way her actions effect him, and his involvement in her sinister plans by default of being confined to her womb give the story a slant unlike any other. <br /><br /><i>Her blood beats through me in thuds like distant artillery fire and I can feel her struggling with a choice. I'm an organ in her body, not separate from her thoughts. I'm party to what she's about to do. When it comes at last, her decision, her whispered command, her single treacherous utterance, appears to issue from my own untried mouth.</i> <br /><br />With its clever approach to narrative, <i>Nutshell</i> is an engaging psychological thriller that's reminiscent of watching Alfred Hithcock's 1954 film, <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=6kCcZCMYw38" rel="nofollow noopener">Rear Window</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17684957881021016398" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2148679247" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2148679247" />
    <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca"><img alt="Jim Fonseca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397669173p2/6743601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2148679247?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Fonseca" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca">Jim Fonseca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=british-authors">british-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2148679247" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7962353703588900973">We know the plot (although not the outcome) from the blurbs and the first chapter. A pregnant woman is having an affair with her husband’s brother. He’s a dreamy type – a poet by trade, if we can consider that a trade, but he has inherited his family house in London worth millions. They plot how and when to kill him. He still loves her but they are separated; he’s living in a dingy apartment. <br/><br/>What gives the book its unique perspective is who is telling the story: the fetus of the pregnant woman.</span>
  <span id="freeText7962353703588900973" style="display:none">We know the plot (although not the outcome) from the blurbs and the first chapter. A pregnant woman is having an affair with her husband’s brother. He’s a dreamy type – a poet by trade, if we can consider that a trade, but he has inherited his family house in London worth millions. They plot how and when to kill him. He still loves her but they are separated; he’s living in a dingy apartment. <br><br>What gives the book its unique perspective is who is telling the story: the fetus of the pregnant woman. Fortunately he’s a bright kid and a quick study. The fetus hears all and knows all. He knows his (her) wine; he can even talk vintages. Sometimes he has a craving for herring. He can feel a book resting on her belly across his back. He learns a lot from radio and from podcasts she listens to on earphones. “I wear my mother like a tight-fitting cap.” <br><br>We learn the house is a dump: “The cleaning lady left in sadness long before my time.” We know how the husband keeps trying to prolong his visits: “His visits don’t end, they fade.” When she makes love with her lover: “This turbulence would shake the wings off a Boeing.” <br><br>On her pregnancy: “Her bare, pink arms and legs, her purple-painted toenails, her full, unarguable beauty are on intimidating display. Her aspect is of a ship of the line, fully though reluctantly rigged, gun hatches lowered. A woman-of-war, of which I’m the bow’s proud figure-head. She descends in floating but intermittent movements. She’ll rise to whatever comes at her.” <br><br>He even has a plan if she gets caught: “I’ll brighten the penumbra of innocence and pathos she’ll want around her. Mother and child – a great religion has spun its best stories around this potent symbol. Sitting on her knee, pointing skywards, I’ll render her immune to prosecution.”<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1507601373i/24131130._SY540_.jpg" width="242" height="343" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>It’s great writing, as we expect from McEwan. He’s a master of analyzing a simple word: The ‘too’ in “I love you too.” Or the ‘now’ in “Personal life wrecked. And now…” <br><br>Thank you to Rebbie for recommending this book to me. <br><br>photo of McEwan from the <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://guardian.com/books">guardian.com/books</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="7962353703588900973" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1692659959" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1692659959" />
    <a title="Roger Brunyate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42294189-roger-brunyate"><img alt="Roger Brunyate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1462877641p2/42294189.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1692659959?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roger Brunyate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roger Brunyate" href="/user/show/42294189-roger-brunyate">Roger Brunyate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42294189-roger-brunyate?shelf=sui-generis">sui-generis</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42294189-roger-brunyate?shelf=comedy-sorta">comedy-sorta</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42294189-roger-brunyate?shelf=fantasy-surreal">fantasy-surreal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42294189-roger-brunyate?shelf=top-ten-2016">top-ten-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42294189-roger-brunyate?shelf=hogarth-shakespeare">hogarth-shakespeare</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1692659959" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10690011153404323788"><b>Hamlet in Utero</b><br/><blockquote>
  <i>Oh God, I could be bounded in a nutshell and count myself king of infinite space—were it not that I have bad dreams.</i>
</blockquote>I could check online, I suppose, but I suspect there is a story here. Is it a coincidence that, within months of the launch of the Hogarth Shakespeare series, in which famous authors (so far Jeanette Winterson, Howard Jacobson, Anne Tyler, and Margaret Atwood) are asked to retell Shakespeare plays in their own words, Ian McEwan (surely the equal of any of them) s</span>
  <span id="freeText10690011153404323788" style="display:none"><b>Hamlet in Utero</b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>Oh God, I could be bounded in a nutshell and count myself king of infinite space—were it not that I have bad dreams.</i>
</blockquote>I could check online, I suppose, but I suspect there is a story here. Is it a coincidence that, within months of the launch of the Hogarth Shakespeare series, in which famous authors (so far Jeanette Winterson, Howard Jacobson, Anne Tyler, and Margaret Atwood) are asked to retell Shakespeare plays in their own words, Ian McEwan (surely the equal of any of them) should come out with his own version of <i>Hamlet</i>, but <i>not</i> part of the series? Was he not asked? Or did he think he would simply do better on his own? Whatever the answer, his novel contains better writing than any of those actually in the series—one might say McEwan's best writing to date, in terms of the brilliance of his handling of words, ideas, and references. In short, an intellectual masterpiece.<br /><br />But as a retelling of the <i>Hamlet</i> story, it strays farther from the canon than even the liberal approach of the other commissions; perhaps that is the point, that he demanded this freedom? Not that this matters; since it is not a Hogarth book, fidelity is no longer a criterion; you can judge it on its own merits. More important to me, though, is whether it tells enough of a story at all—whether Shakespeare's or McEwan's. For the first half or more, I felt that its brilliance as a literary tour-de-force detracted from its value as a <i>novel</i>, in the sense of narrative with rounded characters and a plot. It was not until much later that I changed my mind.<br /><br />For this is Hamlet <i>in utero</i>. Yes, the first line of this 200-page soliloquy is: "So here I am, upside down in a woman." Hamlet's third-trimester interuterine viewpoint may be limited—he has no understanding of colors, for instance—but he more than makes up for it with his other senses; here is is, for example, as an oenophile:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>I like to share a glass with my mother. You may never have experienced, or you will have forgotten, a good burgundy (her favourite) or a good Sancerre (also her favourite) decanted through a healthy placenta. Even before the wine arrives—tonight, a Jean-Max Roger Sancerre—at the sound of a drawn cork, I feel it on my face like the caress of a summer breeze.</i>
</blockquote>Hamlet's (he's not actually named) front-row balcony viewpoint on the intercourse between his mother Trudy and his uncle Claude is bizarrely pornographic, and his knowledge of the outside world, gained ostensibly through his mother's addiction to podcasts, rivals that of the baby Stewie Griffin in <i>Family Guy</i>. This is a very funny book—far more so that McEwan's previous satire, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7140754.Solar" title="Solar by Ian McEwan" rel="nofollow noopener">Solar</a>
</i>—though there were times when I wondered if the author's creation of an all-knowing fetus was simply a device to enable him to pontificate on the state of the world today:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>Free speech no longer free, liberal democracy no longer the obvious port of destiny, robots stealing jobs, liberty in close combat with security, socialism in disgrace, capitalism corrupt, destructive and in disgrace, no alternatives in sight.</i>
</blockquote>Well, let him pontificate now and then. For the brilliance of McEwan's wordplay, his vast range of reference, and his deftness in conjuring almost subliminal Shakespeare echoes throughout is worth the price of a little preaching. And there are times when he is nothing less than beautiful:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>There's pathos in this familiar routine, in the sounds of homely objects touching surfaces. And in the little sigh she makes when she turns or slightly bends our unwieldy form. It's already clear to me how much of life is forgotten even as it happens. Most of it. The unregarded present spooling away from us, the soft tumble of unremarkable thoughts, the long-neglected miracle of existence. When she's no longer twenty-eight and pregnant and beautiful, or even free, she won't remember the way she set down the spoon and the sound it made on slate, the frock she wore today, the touch of her sandal's thong between her toes, the summer's warmth, the white noise of the city beyond the house walls, a short burst of birdsong by a closed window. All gone, already.</i>
</blockquote>I think it was reading this passage on page 162, four-fifths of the way through, that first convinced me that what I was reading was more than a jeu-d'esprit but an often profound meditation. Up until then, there had been that little matter of the story. For the characters, though sharply drawn, could not be said to be rounded, and how much of <i>Hamlet</i> can you tell with the title character still unborn? But then I realized the freedom McEwan had gained by not writing within the Hogarth requirements. We may know how the play turns out, but there is no need for any of it to be replicated in the novel: there need be no revenge plot, no ghost, Hamlet's father need not even be killed. We can read with suspense, because William Shakespeare has no necessary hold over an independent Ian McEwan. In fact, he does not totally depart from the original story, but by the time things get moving in the last fifty pages or so, he manages to create surprises that make the novel increasingly satisfying, not only for style but also for plot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10690011153404323788" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1785703955" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1785703955" />
    <a title="Justin Tate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23082940-justin-tate"><img alt="Justin Tate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593157920p2/23082940.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1785703955?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Justin Tate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Justin Tate" href="/user/show/23082940-justin-tate">Justin Tate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781911214335" href="/book/show/29752912-nutshell">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1785703955" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6885351141651428212">Hamlet in the womb! Literally. The protagonist is a philosophical, wine-loving fetus who gains great intellect from the podcasts his mother listens to.<br/><br/>This is my first time reading Ian McEwan but I'm instantly a fan. His prose is beyond gorgeous, in the same vein as Michael Cunningham who I also worship. Beware that some may consider Ian a "writer's writer" who gets indulgent with his vocabulary and frequently shows off, but with this plot the amplified language works.<br/><br/>It's fresh and evocative to</span>
  <span id="freeText6885351141651428212" style="display:none">Hamlet in the womb! Literally. The protagonist is a philosophical, wine-loving fetus who gains great intellect from the podcasts his mother listens to.<br /><br />This is my first time reading Ian McEwan but I'm instantly a fan. His prose is beyond gorgeous, in the same vein as Michael Cunningham who I also worship. Beware that some may consider Ian a "writer's writer" who gets indulgent with his vocabulary and frequently shows off, but with this plot the amplified language works.<br /><br />It's fresh and evocative to have a fetus thinking at all, but a whole new level having the unborn go off on philosophical rhapsodies and solve a murder mystery before his birth day. Yes, the premise is loose on reality but as a concept piece it's quite a thrill. After all, none of us remember what it was like in the womb. How aware of our surroundings were we really? What would it be like to be highly perceptive and unborn?<br /><br />Again, outstanding accomplishment both in terms of beauty and entertainment. Not easy to write a page-turner that makes you think, but that's precisely what this is. A must-read!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6885351141651428212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2036578761018951144" style="display:none">The narrator is a middle-aged brain trapped inside an unborn baby, itself trapped inside a novel, the events of which the narrator can’t see happening because he's in the dark, much like the reader, but nonetheless, he, the narrator, can recount the events once he’s heard that they’ve happened, and his account is very entertaining even if critical of the crazy plot and unbelievable characters, but unlike the reader, who can abandon the book anytime she wishes, the narrator can’t seem to make up his mind to take the necessary step to escape the plot in which he is encased in spite of the reader urging him to do so over and over until finally he gets his finger out, causing the book to end much to everyone’s relief, narrator, reader and even possibly the author himself.<br><br>That’s it in a <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1474731081i/20636737.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<div id="review_1948103850" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1948103850" />
    <a title="Robin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36555254-robin"><img alt="Robin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536187488p2/36555254.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1948103850?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin" href="/user/show/36555254-robin">Robin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781911214335" href="/book/show/29752912-nutshell">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Robin by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/29843800-bianca">Bianca</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=novella">novella</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=english">english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=2017">2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1948103850" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer508684520646491797"><b>
  <i>Hamlet in utero: daring idea resonates with Bardly brilliance</i>
</b><br/><br/>We all know the basic concept of Hamlet, even if it's been years since we read it in university, or watched Kenneth Branagh's soliloquies (or Mel Gibson, ergh). He's the guy who's fretting constantly, whose fatal flaw is inaction. He can wax poetic like none other, but that's about all he does, bless him.<br/><br/>Well, Ian McEwan had the fantastical idea to resurrect dear Hamlet, in the form of a 3rd trimester foetus. Said foetus (who artic</span>
  <span id="freeText508684520646491797" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>Hamlet in utero: daring idea resonates with Bardly brilliance</i>
</b><br /><br />We all know the basic concept of Hamlet, even if it's been years since we read it in university, or watched Kenneth Branagh's soliloquies (or Mel Gibson, ergh). He's the guy who's fretting constantly, whose fatal flaw is inaction. He can wax poetic like none other, but that's about all he does, bless him.<br /><br />Well, Ian McEwan had the fantastical idea to resurrect dear Hamlet, in the form of a 3rd trimester foetus. Said foetus (who articulates with the sophistication of a middle aged english literature professor and who happens to have the refined tastes of a sommelier) hears his mother and her lover conspire to kill his kindly poet father. What is more helpless than an unborn child? What else can he do but listen, and then soliloquize about the horrors of the world around him, and his powerlessness to act? <br /><br />I bought it. I bought everything. I bought the adult vocabulary bestowed on the baby, I bought the grandiose speeches. I bought the Shakespearean-y plot. I enjoyed every page! It was so very clever, unique, and gorgeously written. It was also funny as hell - I've never before read a scene depicting the experience of a foetus enduring sex between his mother and her lover. Shakespearian quotes are tucked in here and there too, like easter eggs for us to pounce upon with glee. And he even throws in a lil Oedipus for fun. <br /><br />I don't know what rock I've been living under for my entire life, but somehow I've managed to get this far without reading McEwan. I've finally crawled out from under the boulder. Now I've got a lot of catching up to do.</span>
  <a data-text-id="508684520646491797" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10113970626259947498"><b>A unique read but a tad too gimmicky for me.</b> <br/><br/>Nut Shell by Ian McEwan is a story told from the perspective of a foetus. It's a tale of murder and deceit and is clever is its concept and prose.<br/><br/>Trudy has betrayed her husband John. She lives in the marital home which is a priceless London townhouse.   <span id="freeText10113970626259947498" style="display:none"><b>A unique read but a tad too gimmicky for me.</b> <br /><br />Nut Shell by Ian McEwan is a story told from the perspective of a foetus. It's a tale of murder and deceit and is clever is its concept and prose.<br /><br />Trudy has betrayed her husband John. She lives in the marital home which is a priceless London townhouse. She and her lover have a plan and its from the point of view of her 9 month old foetus that we lean what exactly is at foot.<br /><br />I loved the opening line of the Novel " <i> So Here I Am, upside down in a woman. Arms patiently crossed, waiting , waiting and wondering who I'm in, What I'm in for."</i><br /><br />This is my 5th book by McEwan and while I was excited to receive my copy in the post, I had difficulty connecting with this story. I didn't feel I got to know the characters and I was disappointed by the novel's conclusion. Having said that McEwan has some great lines in this book. His prose is impeccable and at 198 pages this reads like a short story but it just was a tad too gimmicky for my liking.<br /><br />My favourite novels by this author are <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/815309.On_Chesil_Beach" title="On Chesil Beach by Ian McEwan" rel="nofollow noopener">On Chesil Beach</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21965107.The_Children_Act" title="The Children Act by Ian McEwan" rel="nofollow noopener">The Children Act</a> <br /><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_1884189735" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1884189735" />
    <a title="Dianne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2947323-dianne"><img alt="Dianne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577914767p2/2947323.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1884189735?book_show_action=true">Jun 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dianne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dianne" href="/user/show/2947323-dianne">Dianne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2947323-dianne?shelf=best-of-2017">best-of-2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1884189735" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11495193173454316601">Brilliant, twisted, witty and sardonic - the story itself seems a more George Saunders concept than Ian McEwan, but it's pure Ian McEwan in execution. The writing!! What language is this he uses to communicate with the reader? I know these words, but how he combines them is so pure, so organic, so perfect and true - I always feel as though I am experiencing the written word for the first time when I read him. Truly, I wish I could shake all the words out of his books and roll in them in pure hed</span>
  <span id="freeText11495193173454316601" style="display:none">Brilliant, twisted, witty and sardonic - the story itself seems a more George Saunders concept than Ian McEwan, but it's pure Ian McEwan in execution. The writing!! What language is this he uses to communicate with the reader? I know these words, but how he combines them is so pure, so organic, so perfect and true - I always feel as though I am experiencing the written word for the first time when I read him. Truly, I wish I could shake all the words out of his books and roll in them in pure hedonistic pleasure.<br /><br />I suspect my favorite book of McEwan's will always be "Atonement," but this is a close second. The story is narrated by an 8-month-old fetus. "Baby," who is very observant and ruminates upon life with the philosophical expanse of Socrates, has overheard his mother and her lover plot to kill his father. Not much baby can do but observe and offer droll commentary, but that is everything! The characters in this domestic drama are wonderfully fleshed out by baby as the plot thickens.<br /><br />Remarkable in every way. I read it twice and know I will pick it up again and again just for the heady pleasure of his immaculate phrasing. Goosebumps, really. We are not worthy.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11495193173454316601" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=mcewan">mcewan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1752000932" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14824841279570965395"><br/>I have rather stormy relationship with Ian McEwan. I quite enjoyed some of his novels while other works, to put it mildly, were a big letdown. The more I read him the more I felt irritated with <i> look at me and admit I’m so fucking brilliant, Ian </i>. He has a knack of picking only topical issues but sometimes I thought he was too clever to his own good; if he hadn’t been so focused on willingness to impress a reader, if he was not so impersonal and allowed the reader to enter his thoughts, if he wa</span>
  <span id="freeText14824841279570965395" style="display:none"><br />I have rather stormy relationship with Ian McEwan. I quite enjoyed some of his novels while other works, to put it mildly, were a big letdown. The more I read him the more I felt irritated with <i> look at me and admit I’m so fucking brilliant, Ian </i>. He has a knack of picking only topical issues but sometimes I thought he was too clever to his own good; if he hadn’t been so focused on willingness to impress a reader, if he was not so impersonal and allowed the reader to enter his thoughts, if he was more in the middle of his own stories I would probably feel more engaged too, I guess. So while, despite all the <i>ifs</i>, I still could acknowledge his skills and dexterity as a writer I was slightly tired with his morbid <i>sex&amp;death</i> fascination and the way he handled it. So surprise, surprise. Who would have thought I would read and enjoy his recent novel so much.<br /><br />I liked the concept of <i>Nutshell</i>, far-fetched as it was it pretty well worked for me. Tragicomic deliberations of nine months foetus, witnessing and involuntarily participating from his relatively safe haven <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ his uncle's dick was a great danger, though  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> in plotting of his mother and uncle to get rid of his father, were clever and quite amusing. To give the voice the muted one, to bestow the power to try change the course of events to the most helpless being was surprisingly refreshing. You can say it's madness, <i>yet there`s method in`t</i>.<br /><br />I liked finding similarity to <i>Hamlet</i> and laughed out loud at times <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ though to tell the truth Trudy reminds more lazy floosie than her regal counterpart Gertrude, uncle Claude is only good at satisfying Trudy’s carnal desire and the kingdom in fact it is a squalid and dirty house, though conveniently located, hence worth some millions  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. While his more sophisticated predecessor could deliberately philosophize to <i>be or not to be</i> our unborn yet narrator had to settle for <i>to kick or not to kick </i> actions for the time being. And though the novel was rather on the eccentric side and our hero strangely verbose for an unborn child, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ months listening to the radio via mother’s womb did its job, I assume  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, I didn’t mind it at all. Some of my previous reservations concerning McEwan’s writing was that with time I had irresistible suspicion it served mostly to shock a reader. But since this one was more or less retelling Shakespearean theme I wasn’t shocked by the ending at all and didn’t expect any change of heart from any part here. I don’t know if that novel could win the prize for the most bizarre or unexpected narrator though for sure it would be among the leaders. But as Bard himself said <i> there are more things in heaven and earth… </i>. And the rest is silence. Or chaos. Your choice.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14824841279570965395" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13500865243347566183">I've got to say that this wasn't one of my favourite McEwan books, but that's not to say I didn't enjoy it. I would still hugely recommend it, mainly because of the narrative voice. The story itself is purely domestic (troubles within a relationship which become sinister *no spoilers*); however, it is told from the perspective of the woman's unborn child. The foetus listens to the world from the womb, deducing the nature of his parents and the world. Since we are a visual-driven culture, this is</span>
  <span id="freeText13500865243347566183" style="display:none">I've got to say that this wasn't one of my favourite McEwan books, but that's not to say I didn't enjoy it. I would still hugely recommend it, mainly because of the narrative voice. The story itself is purely domestic (troubles within a relationship which become sinister *no spoilers*); however, it is told from the perspective of the woman's unborn child. The foetus listens to the world from the womb, deducing the nature of his parents and the world. Since we are a visual-driven culture, this is such a cool way of approaching the world (though, ironically, there is still a huge emphasis on what the child thinks everything will look like). <br /><br />The voice itself, in true McEwan fashion, is highly intelligent and receptive. It draws on impressive reference and profound thought. The depth of thought means I would recommend sitting down and taking the book slowly to fully appreciate it. </span>
