<div id="review_1610444536" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1610444536" />
    <a title="Helle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6909470-helle"><img alt="Helle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430669590p2/6909470.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1610444536?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Helle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Helle" href="/user/show/6909470-helle">Helle</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781911214335" href="/book/show/29752912-nutshell">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=english">english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1610444536" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15827944526738262339">It’s become a bookseller’s cliché to say that it’s an event when Ian McEwan has a new book out. Of course, it’s nonetheless true, and this, his latest novel, was promptly added to my to-read list the minute I heard of it and ordered the minute it came out. That creates pressure, and expectations.<br/><br/>These four days later, I’m not so sure. Certainly <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2095.The_Universe_in_a_Nutshell' title='The Universe in a Nutshell by Stephen Hawking' rel='nofollow noopener'> Nutshell</a> proves McEwan’s skill, if anyone was still in doubt, but within what, I’m just not sure. Shakespearean craziness? Unlikely narrator? Implausibl</span>
  <span id="freeText15827944526738262339" style="display:none">It’s become a bookseller’s cliché to say that it’s an event when Ian McEwan has a new book out. Of course, it’s nonetheless true, and this, his latest novel, was promptly added to my to-read list the minute I heard of it and ordered the minute it came out. That creates pressure, and expectations.<br /><br />These four days later, I’m not so sure. Certainly <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2095.The_Universe_in_a_Nutshell" title="The Universe in a Nutshell by Stephen Hawking" rel="nofollow noopener"> Nutshell</a> proves McEwan’s skill, if anyone was still in doubt, but within what, I’m just not sure. Shakespearean craziness? Unlikely narrator? Implausible omniscience of said narrator? <br /><br />The ‘novel’ is a spin on Shakespeare’s <i>Hamlet</i>, which is most timely since 2016 has marked the 400th anniversary of Shakespeare’s death. So we have (Ger)Trudy as the mother and Claude as the uncle with which Trudy is having an affair – and then we have little (unnamed) Hamlet - <i>in utero</i> - i.e. an unborn foetus, who with his all-knowing verboseness stretches our suspension of disbelief to the point of breakage. Not only are we asked to believe that he picked up his wordsmithing skills while listening – via his mother – to radio 4; we are also meant to buy all the political digressions the foetus makes in the middle of worries about the conspiracy between Trudy and Claude to murder his father - the novel’s ‘story’. <br /><br />This is clearly a construct, then, a conceit even, perhaps deliberately so. McEwan himself has predicted that he would get a kicking for this, but to a large extent reviewers have been positive. The Guardian calls the novel <i>An elegiac masterpiece</i>. The Irish Times suggests it is either <i>ridiculous or rather brilliant</i> (I rather agree) and says it is a return to the darker stories of McEwan’s early career. It is certainly a deviation from his most recent writing (which may be the point), in both story – or lack thereof – and prose (which here resembles poetry more than in any of his other novels). <br /><br />It is difficult to believe in the motivation behind the murder/conspiracy. It is, after all, easier to get a divorce these days than it was in Shakespeare’s time. It is also asking a lot of the reader to believe that a woman at the end of her last trimester will have that much sex (though for all I know, I could be the odd one out in that respect). But these ‘details’ seem to be less important in a novel that never tries to be realistic. The novel, to me, is one long, lyrical, loquacious experiment – a Shakespearean soliloquy in which the core question – to be or not to be (which, cleverly, is never actually asked) - becomes a pre-natal concern rather than that of an adult prince of Denmark. Will he be orphaned, given away, raised in a prison? This is addressed in the first lines already:<br /><br /><i>So here I am, upside down in a woman. Arms patiently crossed, waiting, waiting and wondering who I’m in, what I’m in for.</i><br /><br />Denmark/Elsinore is reduced to a take-away meal consisting of Danish open sandwiches. (Tut tut, says the Dane)<br /><br />Much more can be said and asked about this unusual novel: Is the foetus merely a (too) clever device, acting as McEwan’s mouthpiece? Does the narrative voice deliberately channel Nabokov’s Humbert Humbert? Is there symbolism in the narrator’s father being a poet and thus deserving of death in the minds of lesser characters? Will I appreciate this more on a second reading years hence? <br /><br />Well. Upon closing the book, as I said, I just don’t know. I was impressed but not emotionally engaged. For all these reasons, I want to recommend it to my goodreads friends. I want to see other reactions and find out whether you would land on ‘ridiculous’ or ‘rather brilliant’ – or a combination of both.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15827944526738262339" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1696028341" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1696028341" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1826682-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252295186p2/1826682.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1696028341?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/1826682-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781911214335" href="/book/show/29752912-nutshell">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=crime-caper">crime-caper</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=mysteries">mysteries</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=parenting">parenting</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1696028341" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13312896545561850165">In my mind’s eye is a vision of McEwan himself opening the door to detectives investigating a murder, and noticing everything about what they do, how they look, how their voices sound. He might begin to play on their curiosity a bit, making leading statements that drift off into nothingness…and then suddenly revive his tale with a stronger, quicker tone when they query his lead. Oh, you author of fictions, who plays so with our heads.<br/><br/>Oh course a <i>real</i> murder is not nearly so amusing as its fictio</span>
  <span id="freeText13312896545561850165" style="display:none">In my mind’s eye is a vision of McEwan himself opening the door to detectives investigating a murder, and noticing everything about what they do, how they look, how their voices sound. He might begin to play on their curiosity a bit, making leading statements that drift off into nothingness…and then suddenly revive his tale with a stronger, quicker tone when they query his lead. Oh, you author of fictions, who plays so with our heads.<br /><br />Oh course a <i>real</i> murder is not nearly so amusing as its fictional half-brother, and we get inklings of that in this novel that highlights dark and murky motives for murder from an unusual quarter: a snobbish unborn oenophile who, though he cannot see, has numerous other senses with which to sense and judge his family.<br /><br />One of the best parts of this novel comes right at the start, when McEwan takes a stab at imagining the first moment of consciousness in a human’s life. Not just any human, but Trudy’s son, likewise son of the psoriatic poet, John, whom Trudy plots to kill during the course of this novel. And oh, such lying words spoken to hide the greed, lust, and revenge were never spoken so beautifully, so smoothly, so unbelievably. For they weren’t believed, not by the fetus, the chief inspector, nor, apparently, by John’s poetess friend. <br /><br />This delightful novel had the parallels to Shakespeare’s <i>Hamlet</i> that others have pointed to, but it was also a crime novel par excellence, with the plot, murder, defense, and getaway offered up with sly asides to those of us who think it might be easy to pull off such a thing when one is two weeks shy of a full term.<br /><br />But more, McEwan throws in cameos on the state of the world delivered on TV news shows, radio announcements, or earphones-delivered political podcasts and has our not-yet-born listen to discourse on the torments to come. “These disasters are the work of our twin natures. Clever and infantile.” All of which makes the listening fetus feel anxiety and no little umbrage. “Like everyone else, I’ll take what I want, whatever suits me.” Oh yes, son of mine, you no doubt will.<br /><br />And what of revenge, yearned for by John’s son, who wishes most heartily for John to return and crush the life from his murderers. Following the build-up to the murder while floating in his amniotic sac, our clever fetus realizes he needs to seize his opportunity to avenge his father’s death.<br /><br />We are treated, in the course of this novel, to writing advice: ‘If it doesn’t come at once, it shouldn’t come. There is a special grace in facility.’ ‘Don’t unpack your heart. One detail tells the truth.’ and ‘Form isn’t a cage.’ In <a>an interview</a> McEwan tells us this advice is “rubbish” he picked up from a Saul Bellow interview he’d heard once. "One really does have to work at it,” he tells us and in this case he'd worked on it solidly for 18 months, doing very little else, all the while assailed by doubts about whether he was completely bonkers to be writing from the point of view of a fetus. <br /><br />The book is slim, and yet packed with murderous plotting, references to other literature, the state of the world, and the curious position of a fetus so clearly aware of his environment. This of course would take some writerly skill and attention to detail. An earlier draft of this review sounded patronizing regarding the struggle to birth such a piece, though conceiving a novel from the viewpoint of a fetus was…as easy as rolling off a log, from what McEwan tells us. As it should be.<br /><br />Critics I have read have a tendency to choose favorites from among McEwan oeuvre, utterly discarding a few titles as so much mulch. I feel differently. In nearly every book McEwan brings in world issues we face (like climate change), indicating to me that this author suffers not from a failure of imagination. His sense of humor still reigns supreme, including us in his worldview. He is writing to us, for us. For that we celebrate his skills. <br /><br />------------------<br /><br />This review was modified as a result of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1752359873" rel="nofollow noopener">this GR friend's review</a> which includes links to two interviews with McEwan about this book. <a href="https://www.penguin.co.uk/articles/in-conversation/podcasts/vintage-podcast/2016/margaret-atwood-ian-mcewan/" rel="nofollow noopener">One of the interviews</a> made me rethink and reassess my experience with this novel. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13312896545561850165" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1787077700" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14400610051218656101"><i>Alas poor phœtus! I knew him, McEwan: a fellow<br/>of infinite jest, of most excellent fancy: he hath<br/>borne me in his sac a thousand times; and now, how<br/>abhorred in my imagination it is!</i><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1476763646i/20877357._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='281' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Seriously, Hamlet + 3rd Trimester + Conspiracy + Poetry = funky magic. According to <a href='http://www.nytimes.com/2005/04/15/books/the-plot-thins-or-are-no-stories-new.html?_r=0' rel='nofollow noopener'>Christopher Booker</a>*, <i>"there are only seven basic plots in the whole world -- plots that are recycled again and again in novels, movies, plays and operas."</i> Ian McEwan sucks the Hamlet story right up into the Queen of Denmark's uterus. Not</span>
  <span id="freeText14400610051218656101" style="display:none"><i>Alas poor phœtus! I knew him, McEwan: a fellow<br>of infinite jest, of most excellent fancy: he hath<br>borne me in his sac a thousand times; and now, how<br>abhorred in my imagination it is!</i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1476763646i/20877357._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="281" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Seriously, Hamlet + 3rd Trimester + Conspiracy + Poetry = funky magic. According to <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2005/04/15/books/the-plot-thins-or-are-no-stories-new.html?_r=0" rel="nofollow noopener">Christopher Booker</a>*, <i>"there are only seven basic plots in the whole world -- plots that are recycled again and again in novels, movies, plays and operas."</i> Ian McEwan sucks the Hamlet story right up into the Queen of Denmark's uterus. Not really. This is not Hamlet, rather Hamletesque. I'm going to have to carry this to be or not to be baby through a dreamless night to properly bring her to full-term.<br><br>Ian McEwan seems to have been drinking a lot with Martin Amis. This seems to be almost too clever, but it is so unique that he kinda pulls the little bugger off. Imagine Hamlet soliloquizing inside his mother as his uncle Claude bangs his cock against his mum's thin uterine wall. This is the twisted stuff of literature and art. This is where both dreams and nightmares are born and borne. This novella contains both the spilt seeds of life AND the unfrozen nectar of death. Out of the mouth of unborn babes and placenta-nursing fetuses - tipsy after mum has had her 4th wine - new truths about the world are discovered. I wander, but not far too far; trapped within a membrane, I don't want to give much away.<br><br>*This quote is actually directly from an April 2015, NYTimes review of his book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/114823.The_Seven_Basic_Plots_Why_We_Tell_Stories" title="The Seven Basic Plots Why We Tell Stories by Christopher Booker" rel="nofollow noopener">The Seven Basic Plots: Why We Tell Stories</a> by Michiko Kakutani</span>

<div id="review_1759565019" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1759565019" />
    <a title="PattyMacDotComma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1128612-pattymacdotcomma"><img alt="PattyMacDotComma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577835191p2/1128612.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1759565019?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="PattyMacDotComma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="PattyMacDotComma" href="/user/show/1128612-pattymacdotcomma">PattyMacDotComma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781911214335" href="/book/show/29752912-nutshell">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=aa">aa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=fiction-adult">fiction-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=arc-netgalley-done">arc-netgalley-done</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1759565019" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer887857201897147533">3.5★<br/><br/>Not a fan of this one. I like weird and wonderful if it is somehow believable. It needs to be plausible if we accept certain premises, like when we read a story narrated by someone who’s died. Once we accept the narrator as the actual teller of the story, then we can ‘believe’ the rest of it.<br/><br/>In this case, the narrator is the soon-to-be-born baby still in Trudy’s womb, eavesdropping on her life with her husband's brother, Claude, and their scheming to be free of her husband, John. The baby re</span>
  <span id="freeText887857201897147533" style="display:none">3.5★<br /><br />Not a fan of this one. I like weird and wonderful if it is somehow believable. It needs to be plausible if we accept certain premises, like when we read a story narrated by someone who’s died. Once we accept the narrator as the actual teller of the story, then we can ‘believe’ the rest of it.<br /><br />In this case, the narrator is the soon-to-be-born baby still in Trudy’s womb, eavesdropping on her life with her husband's brother, Claude, and their scheming to be free of her husband, John. The baby refers to itself as he/him.<br /><br />Okay. I’ll accept his point-of-view just as I’d accept a fly-on-the-wall point of view. <br /><br />The baby listens in and tells us how Claude has won his mother over with noisy, vigorous, messy sex (much to the disgust of her nearby, uncomfortably prodded, offspring).<br /><br />Okay. A bit unappealing, but okay. Then the baby reminds us that he can’t see things (well, duh, we KNOW that), but he tries to piece together what information there is to create a story. <br /><br /><b>
  <i>“When I hear ‘blue’, which I’ve never seen, I imagine some kind of mental event that’s fairly close to ‘green’—which I’ve never seen. . . I am, or I was, despite what the geneticists are no saying, a blank slate. But a slippery, porous slate no schoolroom or cottage roof could find use for, a slate that writes upon itself as it grows by the day and becomes less blank.”</i>
</b><br /><br />Not okay. This bub has apparently listened to his father’s poetry and literary conversations, so we could credit him with understanding that the words ‘blue’ and ‘green’ have some connection to each other. But to carry on with the descriptive metaphor of himself as a ‘slate’ in reference to a schoolroom or a cottage roof is outside the premise I’ve accepted. <br /><br />I’ve accepted this tiny being can relate a story to me (ridiculous, but, you know, I agreed), but I can’t accept that on the one hand he tells me (many times) that he can’t see or smell, and on the other hand can create metaphors and later describe how he imagines a young woman visitor one evening:<br /><br /><b>
  <i>“By nocturnal association I dress her in tight-fitting black leather jacket and jeans, let her be young, pale, pretty, her own woman.”</i>
</b><br /><br />There’s plenty of musing about how many glasses of white wine Trudy’s drinking and how ‘relaxed’ the baby is becoming. In fact, when she’s drinking a lovely local white in NZ, the baby says:<br /><br /><b>
  <i>“I’m feeling a friendly touch of disassociation: I’m already some useful steps removed and see myself revealed some fifteen feet below me, like a fallen climber spreadeagled and supine on a rock. I can begin to comprehend my situation, I can think as well as feel. An unassuming New World white can do this much.”</i>
</b><br /><br />Way too far off track from the first premise I accepted, I'm afraid. There are also a fair few political messages about the world today that seem out of place, except that this is a baby wondering what sort of world he’d about to enter, I suppose.<br /><br />Odd and not really my taste, I’m afraid. But McEwan is always worth reading to see what he’s up to. <br /><br />Thanks to NetGalley and Random House UK for a preview copy.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="887857201897147533" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2070320317" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer5160402733823341119">My last experience of reading McEwan was <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/815309.On_Chesil_Beach' title='On Chesil Beach by Ian McEwan' rel='nofollow noopener'>On Chesil Beach</a> which I found rather dreary and depressing and deterred me from reading his subsequent novels. Encouraged by the positive reviews, I have had this one on the shelf since the paperback came out last summer but have only just found the time to read it.<br/><br/>This is a book that really should not work, but it is a lively and enjoyable read full of surprises. Its central conceit - transplanting part of the story of Hamlet to modern Britain and making</span>
  <span id="freeText5160402733823341119" style="display:none">My last experience of reading McEwan was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/815309.On_Chesil_Beach" title="On Chesil Beach by Ian McEwan" rel="nofollow noopener">On Chesil Beach</a> which I found rather dreary and depressing and deterred me from reading his subsequent novels. Encouraged by the positive reviews, I have had this one on the shelf since the paperback came out last summer but have only just found the time to read it.<br /><br />This is a book that really should not work, but it is a lively and enjoyable read full of surprises. Its central conceit - transplanting part of the story of Hamlet to modern Britain and making the narrator an unborn baby is really just a twist on the traditional omniscient narrator and McEwan clearly had fun deciding what the narrator should be allowed to know, but he never addresses why he has an innate knowledge of language or such a wide range of allusion. All forgiveable - this is an entertaining exercise and a short book that never outstays its welcome.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15059268585159221084">Update one week post reading: So, I am seeing now that this book is a re-telling of Hamlet!?!? How did I miss that!?<br/><br/>4 to 4.5 stars<br/><br/>Seems like this offering from McEwan tends not to get very high ratings, but I liked it quite a bit. It was more of a novella than a novel, but that is what I needed right now – a quick and enjoyable read!<br/><br/>The key to this book is that it is told from the perspective of an in-utero fetus with the intelligence of an adult. As I mentioned in one of my comments while I was</span>
  <span id="freeText15059268585159221084" style="display:none">Update one week post reading: So, I am seeing now that this book is a re-telling of Hamlet!?!? How did I miss that!?<br><br>4 to 4.5 stars<br><br>Seems like this offering from McEwan tends not to get very high ratings, but I liked it quite a bit. It was more of a novella than a novel, but that is what I needed right now – a quick and enjoyable read!<br><br>The key to this book is that it is told from the perspective of an in-utero fetus with the intelligence of an adult. As I mentioned in one of my comments while I was reading, this is what I pictured the entire time:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1579202162i/28789858.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In order to accept this story, you must suspend your disbelief and be comfortable with a fetus talking to you like a scholar. But what this does is make the story very unique. Only events happening within the vicinity of the mother can be interpreted – anything else is conjecture. Also, without this viewpoint and approach, it is just another story of lust and revenge. With this narrator, it makes it a special experience.<br><br>I have refrained from reading any reviews so far so as I write this, I cannot say for sure why the star rating is trending low. For me it was easily readable and entertaining. He does get a bit long winded at points – and, with the vocabulary used, maybe those who are unable to suspend disbelief far enough are not into it. Also, some of the directions the plot takes might make people too uncomfortable <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ One possibility: sex from the viewpoint of the fetus. Another one: When the fetus attempts suicide to foil the plans of his mother <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>In the case of this book, I enjoyed it but I am not sure I can recommend it. It is just too hard to say who would like it and who wouldn’t.<br><br>Follow up: After writing this I went and looked at the ratings and reviews. It appears it is not my Goodreads friends responsible for the 3.67 rating (at the time of this review)! Mainly 4 and 5 stars!</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1806224418">

<div id="review_1771238969" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1771238969" />
    <a title="Seemita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34622220-seemita"><img alt="Seemita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473185997p2/34622220.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1771238969?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seemita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seemita" href="/user/show/34622220-seemita">Seemita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781911214335" href="/book/show/29752912-nutshell">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=toi">toi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=monologue">monologue</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=uk">uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=europe">europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=singing_words">singing_words</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1771238969" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3464560231496765415"><i>[Originally appeared here (with edits): <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://timesofindia.indiatimes.com/life-style/books/features/Review-Nutshell/articleshow/55116264.cms'>http://timesofindia.indiatimes.com/li...</a>]</i> <i>
  <blockquote>Pessimism is too easy, even delicious, the badge and plume of intellectuals everywhere. It absolves the thinking classes of solutions.</blockquote>
</i> This wonderfully sapient insight springs somewhere in the middle of this book and almost gives away the rationale behind McEwan’s choice of protagonist - a <b>fetus.</b><br/><br/>Yes, this 200-odd pages of scheming a murder is seen through the eyes of a fetus from the womb of his mother, a party</span>
  <span id="freeText3464560231496765415" style="display:none"><i>[Originally appeared here (with edits): <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://timesofindia.indiatimes.com/life-style/books/features/Review-Nutshell/articleshow/55116264.cms">http://timesofindia.indiatimes.com/li...</a>]</i> <i>
  <blockquote>Pessimism is too easy, even delicious, the badge and plume of intellectuals everywhere. It absolves the thinking classes of solutions.</blockquote>
</i> This wonderfully sapient insight springs somewhere in the middle of this book and almost gives away the rationale behind McEwan’s choice of protagonist - a <b>fetus.</b><br /><br />Yes, this 200-odd pages of scheming a murder is seen through the eyes of a fetus from the womb of his mother, a party to the hatching game. The other party is her lover, who is also incidentally her husband’s brother. They huddle together in the former’s house, conspiring to kill the husband. Sounds familiar?<br /><br />Those who have read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1420.Hamlet" title="Hamlet by William Shakespeare" rel="nofollow noopener">Hamlet</a> would clearly jump the gun and arrest McEwan in plagiaristic contempt. But lo and behold! The man has chosen the plot deliberately, much as a tribute to the Bard and accomplishes the job with mesmerizing wizardry. For McEwan dons one of his shrewdest and most luscious hats to undertake this arduous task of retelling the Shakespearean spell-binder and produces a cunning, stylized, original yet deep version of it.<br /><br />The fetus has keen sensorial abilities and McEwan grants him an endearing, occasionally hilarious, armoury of language to narrate his observations. <blockquote>
  <i> I picture a hayloft, off which a hundred-kilo sack of grain is tossed to the granary floor. Then another, and a third. Such are the thuds of my mother’s heart. </i>
</blockquote> The musty air that whooshes past him, tell him his mother has opened a window to an overcast sky, and from the soft thuds of her arm muscles, he envisages her to be slowly getting down the stairs. Her night walks, her lovemaking, her alcohol binge, her brave facades, they all find a delightful reflection on the fetus’ world, as if there was a clear mirror that reflected, without dilution, the mother’s acts and thoughts, to the son.<br /><br />But the obedient son must reunite his parents and stop the heinous crime from being committed. But what can a mere fetus, who can’t even breathe of his own volition, possibly do to avert the catastrophe?<br /><br />This book restored my love-hate relationship with McEwan to a respectable balance as I happily awarded numerous brownie points to him. Choosing an unconventional hero is easy; proving him one, is difficult. While many authors would have dispatched the proposition of appointing a fetus as a narrator to dustbin without much of a thought, McEwan stays true to this absolutely refreshing point of view with a vengeance. Not jumbling up the adult vagaries with the fetus’ across most of pages is a feat which demands more than few claps. But that the modern-day child, genetically, has a sharper IQ than his parent is unleashed without restraint. <blockquote>
  <i> It wasn’t hatred that killed the innocents but faith, that famished ghost, still revered, even in the mildest quarters. Long ago, someone pronounced groundless certainty a virtue. Now, the politest people say it is.</i>
</blockquote> At every instance that I contemplated reducing McEwan’s marks for imparting an astoundingly (often unbelievably) fecund tongue to a 8-months old fetus, he blurred that rationale with a blizzard of hypnotic writing. <a href="https://www.thestar.com/entertainment/2016/10/01/ian-mcewan-the-connection-between-hamlet-and-his-fetus-narrator.html" rel="nofollow noopener">(He called it later, a liberating act)</a> Of course, they remained far from being all shallow and thus, I remained on course to see where the fetus lands up, literally. The climax, where things could have gone awry, held up good and the multiple inferences sitting hidden in the fetus’ journey assumed another delectable layer of story-telling. <br /><br />Should McEwan bring a sequel to this book, he can be assured of atleast one reader.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3464560231496765415" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1777664144" />
    <a title="Barbara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2172096-barbara"><img alt="Barbara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598060221p2/2172096.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1777664144?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barbara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barbara" href="/user/show/2172096-barbara">Barbara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1777664144" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10754684038603520335"><br/>In Nutshell, a sort of modern take on 'Hamlet', a son becomes aware that his mother Trudy and her lover Claude are planning to murder his father John - who happens to be Claude's brother. The twist in Ian McEwan's novel is that the son, and narrator of the book, is a late term fetus.....in utero. <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572352949i/28369683.png' alt='' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>The not-yet-born baby, who's preternaturally knowledgeable and articulate, explains that he got his smarts from overheard conservations and the many podcasts his mother listens to. (The descriptions of </span>
  <span id="freeText10754684038603520335" style="display:none"><br>In Nutshell, a sort of modern take on 'Hamlet', a son becomes aware that his mother Trudy and her lover Claude are planning to murder his father John - who happens to be Claude's brother. The twist in Ian McEwan's novel is that the son, and narrator of the book, is a late term fetus.....in utero. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572352949i/28369683.png" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The not-yet-born baby, who's preternaturally knowledgeable and articulate, explains that he got his smarts from overheard conservations and the many podcasts his mother listens to. (The descriptions of the podcasts alone make the book worth reading. LOL)<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572352949i/28369687.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Trudy is separated from her husband John but continues to live in the London home he inherited from his parents, while hubby languishes in a small apartment. The family property is worth millions of pounds, and the greedy adulterers plan to kill John, sell the house, and reap the rewards. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572352949i/28369689.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>To add insult to injury, Claude has mentioned 'placing the baby somewhere' after the murder - so the couple can go on their merry way unencumbered. Naturally, this doesn't sit right with the unborn infant.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572352949i/28369685.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Trudy and Claude think John - a rather dreamy poet - is unaware of their liaison. To the adulterers dismay, John shows up unexpectedly one day and disabuses them of this notion. John tells his wife and brother that he's moving back into the house, and they have to leave. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572352949i/28369686.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Taken aback, the adulterers decide to accelerate their murder plot. That's about all I can say about the story without spoilers. <br><br>The eavesdropping fetus is quite a hoot. He's like a tiny sommelier - very smart about wine - and tipsy half the time from Trudy's drinking. The infant is also savvy about sex, and privy to lots of hot coupling between Trudy and Claude. As a result, the baby frequently worries about his uncle's wiener poking (and spraying) his head.....thinking he might absorb some of Claude's (unwanted) characteristics. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572352949i/28369684.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In fact, the poor baby has a lot to worry about as he ruminates about everything he overhears. So....concerned for his personal welfare, the fetus takes matters into his own hands at the book's climax. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572352949i/28369688.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I enjoyed this unique story and would recommend the book to fans of literary novels, and readers looking for something a little different.<br><br>You can follow my reviews at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://reviewsbybarbsaffer.blogspot.com/">https://reviewsbybarbsaffer.blogspot....</a></span>

<div id="review_1843018225" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1843018225" />
    <a title="Julie Christine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1213607-julie-christine"><img alt="Julie Christine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445900882p2/1213607.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1843018225?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie Christine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie Christine" href="/user/show/1213607-julie-christine">Julie Christine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=best-of-2017">best-of-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=mystery-crime-thriller">mystery-crime-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=read-2017">read-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=british-isles-theme-setting">british-isles-theme-setting</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1843018225" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11811220503100761224">In the time it took me to write this review, Ian McEwan has written at least one more novel. I mean, seriously. Dude is prolific. Also brilliant. Deliciously dark and witty and, dammit. Even with his novels that I can't say I <i>like</i>, I never cease to marvel at what he can do with same words we all have at our disposal. <br/><br/>A master of restraint and brevity, McEwan's short works are perhaps his most astonishing literary achievements, and his latest, <i>Nutshell</i>, is a twisted wee delight.<br/><br/>The tale is narrat</span>
  <span id="freeText11811220503100761224" style="display:none">In the time it took me to write this review, Ian McEwan has written at least one more novel. I mean, seriously. Dude is prolific. Also brilliant. Deliciously dark and witty and, dammit. Even with his novels that I can't say I <i>like</i>, I never cease to marvel at what he can do with same words we all have at our disposal. <br /><br />A master of restraint and brevity, McEwan's short works are perhaps his most astonishing literary achievements, and his latest, <i>Nutshell</i>, is a twisted wee delight.<br /><br />The tale is narrated by a erudite fetus (yep, fetus. Or rather, <i>foetus</i>, since we're in Britain now, Toto) who has amassed a wealth of knowledge about wine, a firm grasp on history, and a keen awareness of current events all by listening to the world beyond the womb, mostly to the podcasts that carry his mother through nights of insomnia.<br /><br />Baby is also a possible Witness for the Prosecution, after being in utero privy to the murder plot simmering away between his mother and her lover, who also happens to be the brother of Baby's father. I know, RIGHT?<br /><br />Trust me. The premise sounds redunkulous, but we're in capable hands here. Riffing on Hamlet, McEwan takes a fiendish, outlandish, and tawdry story and creates something fresh, taut, relevant and often hilarious. He plays to our darkest sensibilities, shocking and awing in the most satisfying ways. <br /><br />I'm being deliberately opaque about the plot. I gave you the premise, that's enough. Whether or not the wispy Helen and her buffoonish lover carry out and carry off their scheme is something you'll have to discover on your own. And oh goodness, you just should. You should read this. And that's my review in a nutshell.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11811220503100761224" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1727995183" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1727995183" />
    <a title="Ron Charles" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1384944-ron-charles"><img alt="Ron Charles" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567601289p2/1384944.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1727995183?book_show_action=true">Aug 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ron Charles" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ron Charles" href="/user/show/1384944-ron-charles">Ron Charles</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781911214335" href="/book/show/29752912-nutshell">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1727995183" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17211532563065836648">“Nutshell,” Ian McEwan’s preposterously weird little novel, is more brilliant than it has any right to be. The plot sounds like something sprung from a drunken round of literary Mad Libs: a crime of passion based on Shakespeare’s “Hamlet,” narrated by a fetus.<br/><br/><i>That it should come to this!</i><br/><br/>If you can get beyond that icky premise, you’ll discover a novel that sounds like a lark but offers a story that’s surprisingly suspenseful, dazzlingly clever and gravely profound. To the extent that “Hamlet” is </span>
  <span id="freeText17211532563065836648" style="display:none">“Nutshell,” Ian McEwan’s preposterously weird little novel, is more brilliant than it has any right to be. The plot sounds like something sprung from a drunken round of literary Mad Libs: a crime of passion based on Shakespeare’s “Hamlet,” narrated by a fetus.<br /><br /><i>That it should come to this!</i><br /><br />If you can get beyond that icky premise, you’ll discover a novel that sounds like a lark but offers a story that’s surprisingly suspenseful, dazzlingly clever and gravely profound. To the extent that “Hamlet” is an existential tragedy marked with moments of comedy, “Nutshell” is a philosophical comedy marked by. . . . .<br /><br /><i>To read the rest of this review, go to The Washington Post:</i><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/entertainment/books/ian-mcewans-nutshell-a-tale-of-betrayal-and-murder-as-told-by-a-fetus/2016/09/12/8b31ba22-7694-11e6-b786-19d0cb1ed06c_story.html">https://www.washingtonpost.com/entert...</a><br /><br /><i>And to see The Totally Hip Video Book Review, click here:</i><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/video/entertainment/ron-charles-shakespeare-in-utero/2016/09/12/f35e2aa2-78fd-11e6-8064-c1ddc8a724bb_video.html">https://www.washingtonpost.com/video/...</a> </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1884077884" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1884077884" />
    <a title="Margitte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7481492-margitte"><img alt="Margitte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361772481p2/7481492.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1884077884?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Margitte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Margitte" href="/user/show/7481492-margitte">Margitte</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/29929898-nutshell">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=2016-releases">2016-releases</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=british-author">british-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=british-novels">british-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=suspense">suspense</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=literary-novel">literary-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=2017-read">2017-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=murder">murder</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1884077884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14205835339219677087">A wildly snobbish neonatal persona, Fetus Cairncross, introduces himself to the world in no uncertain terms:<blockquote>
  <i>"So here I am, upside down in a woman. Arms patiently crossed, waiting, waiting and wondering who I’m in, what I’m in for. My eyes close nostalgically when I remember how I once drifted in my translucent body bag, floated dreamily in the bubble of my thoughts through my private ocean in slow-motion somersaults, colliding gently against the transparent bounds of my confinement, the confi</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText14205835339219677087" style="display:none">A wildly snobbish neonatal persona, Fetus Cairncross, introduces himself to the world in no uncertain terms:<blockquote>
  <i>"So here I am, upside down in a woman. Arms patiently crossed, waiting, waiting and wondering who I’m in, what I’m in for. My eyes close nostalgically when I remember how I once drifted in my translucent body bag, floated dreamily in the bubble of my thoughts through my private ocean in slow-motion somersaults, colliding gently against the transparent bounds of my confinement, the confiding membrane that vibrated with, even as it muffled, the voices of conspirators in a vile enterprise. That was in my careless youth. Now, fully inverted, not an inch of space to myself, knees crammed against belly, my thoughts as well as my head are fully engaged. I’ve no choice, my ear is pressed all day and night against the bloody walls. I listen, make mental notes, and I’m troubled. I’m hearing pillow talk of deadly intent and I’m terrified by what awaits me, by what might draw me in."</i>
</blockquote> He stays updated on the news of the world, by kicking his mother during long sleeping quiet hours so that she wakes up, become insomniac and switch on the radio to listen to the news. <i>"And she likes podcast lectures, and self-improving audio books—Know Your Wine in fifteen parts, biographies of seventeenth-century playwrights, and various world classics. James Joyce’s</i> Ulysses<i> sends her to sleep, even as it thrills me. When, in the early days, she inserted her earbuds, I heard clearly, so efficiently did sound waves travel through jawbone and clavicle, down through her skeletal structure, swiftly through the nourishing amniotic."</i><br /><br />He shares a Bachus infatuation with his mother.<blockquote>
  <i>"I like to share a glass with my mother. You may never have experienced, or you will have forgotten, a good burgundy (her favourite) or a good Sancerre (also her favourite) decanted through a healthy placenta. Even before the wine arrives—tonight, a Jean-Max Roger Sancerre—at the sound of a drawn cork, I feel it on my face like the caress of a summer breeze. I know that alcohol will lower my intelligence. It lowers everybody’s intelligence. But oh, a joyous, blushful Pinot Noir, or a gooseberried Sauvignon, sets me turning and tumbling across my secret sea, reeling off the walls of my castle, the bouncy castle that is my home. Or so it did when I had more space. Now I take my pleasures sedately, and by the second glass my speculations bloom with that licence whose name is poetry. My thoughts unspool in well-sprung pentameters, end-stopped and run-on lines in pleasing variation. But she never takes a third, and it wounds me. “I have to think of baby,” I hear her say as she covers her glass with a priggish hand. That’s when I have it in mind to reach for my oily cord, as one might a velvet rope in a well-staffed country house, and pull sharply for service. What ho! Another round here for us friends!</i>
</blockquote>Ian McEwan took it upon himself to join the 400 year Shakespeare celebrations with his book <i>Nutshell</i> in which he adds a large dollop of clever wit and (often boringly preachy) commentary on the current state of affairs in the world. His ingenious use of a fetus as Hamlet is delightful, hilarious and brilliant.<br /><br />Here is a short summary of Hamlet: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<i>"Prince Hamlet is depressed. Having been summoned home to Denmark from school in Germany to attend his father's funeral, he is shocked to find his mother Gertrude already remarried. The Queen has wed Hamlet's Uncle Claudius, the dead king's brother. To Hamlet, the marriage is "foul incest." Worse still, Claudius has had himself crowned King despite the fact that Hamlet was his father's heir to the throne. Hamlet suspects foul play.<br /><br />When his father's ghost visits the castle, Hamlet's suspicions are confirmed. The Ghost complains that he is unable to rest in peace because he was murdered. Claudius, says the Ghost, poured poison in King Hamlet's ear while the old king napped. Unable to confess and find salvation, King Hamlet is now consigned, for a time, to spend his days in Purgatory and walk the earth by night. He entreats Hamlet to avenge his death, but to spare Gertrude, to let Heaven decide her fate."</i> (source:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.cliffsnotes.com/literature/h/hamlet/play-summary">https://www.cliffsnotes.com/literatur...</a> ) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Fetus Cairncross is facing the same fate as dearly beloved Hamlet: his mother and Claude the fraud, the <i>Dull-brained yokel</i> her lover in a hot relationship, plotting the murder of his father to take possession of his wealth. The baby stands to lose his inheritance if this devious pair (his mother Trudy, and her lover, the father's brother), succeed with their plans. Claude's existence denies Fetus Cairncross his rightful claim to a happy life in the care of both parents. Claude is not even a smiling rogue, or a colorful chancer. Instead <i>"he is dull do the point of brilliance, vapid beyond invention, his banality as finely wrought as the arabesque of the Blue Mosque.</i>" His whistling and speaking is like a twin torment to the dear fetus. <i>"Anxiously, I finger my cord. It serves for worry beads.</i><br /><br />Sooooo ... Fetus is planning his revenge, although he is marred by the moral dilemma of hurting his mother whom he loves as much as he despises her. He also endangers his own life should his plans go belly up!<br /><br />Oh the pathos! The tragedy!<br /><br />COMMENTS<br /><br />Hamlet in Utero.<br /><br />I cannot but love this book, although the consistent sexual content quickly became stale bread and not funny anymore. Add to that the dumping of words in monologue after monologue and I had myself listening to the scratching of white chalk... scratching...scratching...scratching... Arggggghh! <br /><br />In the spirit of Hamlet who cannot stop thinking, our sophisticated eloquent Fetus Cairncross follows the same pattern of thought as Hamlet,that almost drives him to insanity and mayhem. <i>"Not everyone knows what it is like to have your father’s rival’s penis inches from your nose.</i> And they don't even refrain at this late stage on behalf of <i>in utero</i> Hamlet! <blockquote><i>"Courtesy, if not clinical judgement, demands it. I close my eyes, I grit my gums, I brace myself against the uterine walls. This turbulence would shake the wings of a Boeing. My mother whips him on with her fairground shrieks. Wall of Death! On each occasion, on every piston stroke, I dread that he'll break and shaft my soft-bone skull and seed my thoughts with his essence, with the teeming cream of his banality. Then, brain-damaged, I'll think and speak like him. I'll be the son of of Claude.<br /><br />But rather trap me inside a wingless Boeing's mid-Atlantic plunge than book me one more night of his foreplay.</i>"</blockquote>This book, apart from the vulgar-ish interludes, are as quotable as it gets. And what a great celebration of Hamlet it is. Of all the books involved in the contemporary Shakespearean festivities, this one grabbed my attention the most, based on the different reviews of the different authors's books involved. Ian McEwan was not one of the invitees of the Hogarth project, but independently did a great job.<br /><br />SOME Festival tidbits YOU CAN SKIP<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />2016 was the year in which the death of Shakespeare, four hundred years ago have been commemorated all over the world. <br /><br />All artistic expressions were used for the purpose, including, for instance, Q Brothers, a hip hop group who entertained 4,500 people in Chicago with their performance <i>Orthello: The Remix.</i> Graphic artist designed GIF with famous quotes; <br />Dramas such as <i>A murder in Venice. A wolf at the door.</i> inspired by Shakespeare: <i>The Wolf in the Water</i> by Naomi Aldermanm etc. All over the world everyone went big or went home, sort of!<br /><br />Authors such as William Shakespeare and Cervantes(who were both celebrated together in many parts of the world on their deaths 400 years ago) have taken on such a cultural significance, that contemporary artists and writers still engage with the literature of the 16th and early 17th century.<br /><br />Various authors were asked to rewrite Shakespeare in their own words, probably in the hope to rekindle new life for new generations into the work of the Britain's greatest playwright, poet and author.<br /><br />The Hogarth Project invited bestselling authors to 'refresh' Shakespeare in their own words:<br /><blockquote><i>Hag-Seed</i> by Margaret Atwood (The Tempest) - Published October 11th 2016;<br /><br /><i>The Gap of Time</i> by Jeanette Winterson (The Winter's Tale)- Published October 6th 2015;<br /><br /><i>Vinegar Girl</i> by Anne Tyler(The Taming of the Shrew)- Published June 21st 2016;<br /><br /><i>New Boy</i> by Tracy Chevalier (The Tragedy of Othello, the Moor of Venice) - Expected publication: May 11th 2017;<br /><br /><i>Shylock is my Name</i> - Howard Jacobson - (The Merchant of Venice) - Published February 9th 2016;<br /><br />Jo Nesbo’s retelling of Macbeth is coming in 2017!<br />Edward St. Aubyn’s retelling of King Lear is coming in 2018!<br />Gillian Flynn’s retelling of Hamlet is coming in 2021!<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://hogarthshakespeare.com/">http://hogarthshakespeare.com/</a></blockquote> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Long Live Shakespeare the Great! May his works survive another 400 years even if the planet won't make it! Prost! Cheers! Gesundheit! Salud! A la tienne to your memory! <br /><br />I think you get it. This was good! :-) <br /><br />And here's to Ian McEwan's masterful wordsmithery!</span>
  <a data-text-id="14205835339219677087" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1775268905" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4238804724834568786">“To be bound in a nutshell, see the world in two inches of ivory, in a grain of sand. Why not, when all of literature, all of art, of human endeavour, is just a speck in the universe of possible things.” <br/>― Ian McEwan, Nutshell: A Novel<br/><br/><br/>Ahhh! I want to squeal in sheer delight at this book! I've never read a book this genius before, and especially not one from a writer who is deeply self-aware but also wonderfully modest at the same time. <br/><br/>The line I quoted is just one of a seemingly endless number </span>
  <span id="freeText4238804724834568786" style="display:none">“To be bound in a nutshell, see the world in two inches of ivory, in a grain of sand. Why not, when all of literature, all of art, of human endeavour, is just a speck in the universe of possible things.” <br />― Ian McEwan, Nutshell: A Novel<br /><br /><br />Ahhh! I want to squeal in sheer delight at this book! I've never read a book this genius before, and especially not one from a writer who is deeply self-aware but also wonderfully modest at the same time. <br /><br />The line I quoted is just one of a seemingly endless number of lines that could perfectly illustrate the intelligence, deep-thinking wit and humor that this little baby boy possesses in utero.<br /><br />If only we could all manage to be so enlightened in the face of the situation he's in: being forced to witness his mother's plans to murder her husband (his father). She and her brother-in-law have the same goal in mind: do away with the baby's father. I won't say more for fear of spoiling it for you.<br /><br />Hopefully Ian McEwan will write a sequel. I'm already desperate to read more of the baby's candor and perceptions of the precarious situations he finds himself in, even though the book is still a pretty new release.<br /><br />Please Ian McKewan, write more about him, we can't get enough. But. ;)<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4238804724834568786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1756359352" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1756359352" />
    <a title="Bam cooks the books ;-)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5922426-bam-cooks-the-books--"><img alt="Bam cooks the books ;-)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559653145p2/5922426.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1756359352?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bam cooks the books ;-)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bam cooks the books ;-)" href="/user/show/5922426-bam-cooks-the-books--">Bam cooks the books ;-)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5922426-bam-cooks-the-books--?shelf=2016-reading-challenge">2016-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5922426-bam-cooks-the-books--?shelf=2016-aty-reading-challenge">2016-aty-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5922426-bam-cooks-the-books--?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5922426-bam-cooks-the-books--?shelf=i-own-it">i-own-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5922426-bam-cooks-the-books--?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5922426-bam-cooks-the-books--?shelf=doubleday-facebook-reading-group">doubleday-facebook-reading-group</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1756359352" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6795761060175000292">#2016-aty-reading-challenge-week-49: a book with a great opening line. "So here I am, upside down in a woman. Arms patiently crossed, waiting, waiting, waiting, and wondering who I'm in, what I'm in for." Actually, the entire first paragraph is tremendous--the plot told through the point of view of a fetus. <br/><br/>And this is no ordinary fetus. This one has been listening to podcasts through his mother's earbuds and already knows a great deal about world events, politics, environmental concerns, etc. A</span>
  <span id="freeText6795761060175000292" style="display:none">#2016-aty-reading-challenge-week-49: a book with a great opening line. "So here I am, upside down in a woman. Arms patiently crossed, waiting, waiting, waiting, and wondering who I'm in, what I'm in for." Actually, the entire first paragraph is tremendous--the plot told through the point of view of a fetus. <br /><br />And this is no ordinary fetus. This one has been listening to podcasts through his mother's earbuds and already knows a great deal about world events, politics, environmental concerns, etc. And he also knows his mother and his uncle are plotting to kill his father, a la the story of Hamlet. In fact, the book begins with a quote from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1420.Hamlet" title="Hamlet by William Shakespeare" rel="nofollow noopener">Hamlet</a> from which apparently the title springs: "Oh God, I could be bounded in a nutshell and count myself a king of infinite space--were it not that I have bad dreams."<br /><br />Suspend your disbelief and give this latest book by Ian McEwan a try. What a unique perspective from which to spin a wicked tale of murder!<br /><br />I was very fortunate to win a copy of this book from Doubleday through the Keep Turning Pages reading group giveaway--many thanks to them! I rank Ian McEwan as one of my favorite authors; he never disappoints. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6795761060175000292" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('IOr3f7e083whe+0QL9VK9aSRRNGNbvSLH53wIuKz3ekazJ6tDOo6FIZQWXHYIEAqcs6jGUPjeuxE47aTL/Avcw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

Taunted and bullied, he is also isolated by his intelligence and wit, and his unique circumstances: an unbaptized boy raised by a single mother in a fiercely Catholic society. Soon on the cusp of teenagehood, Percy is filled with yearning, wild with hormones, and longing for what he can’t have—wanting to be let in...and let out. At the top of his wish list is his disturbingly alluring mother, Penelope, whose sex appeal fairly leaps off the page. Everyone in St. John’s lusts after her—including her sister-in-law, Medina; their paying boarder, the local chemistry teacher, Pops MacDougal; and...Percy. Percy, Penelope, and Pops live in the Mount, home of the city’s Catholic schools and most of its clerics, none of whom are overly fond of the scandalous Joyces despite the seemingly benign protection of the Archbishop of Newfoundland himself, whose chief goal is to bring “little Percy Joyce” into the bosom of the Church by whatever means necessary. In pursuit of that goal, Brother McHugh, head of Percy’s school, sets out to uncover the truth behind what he senses to be the complicated relationships of the Joyce household. And indeed there are dark secrets to be kept hidden: Pops is in love with Penelope, but Penelope and Medina are also in love—an illegal relationship: if caught, they will be sent to the Mental, and Percy, already an outcast of society, will be left without a family.  The Son of a Certain Woman brilliantly mixes sorrow and laughter as it builds toward an unforgettable ending. Will Pops marry Penelope? Will Penelope and Medina be found out? Will Percy be lured into the Church? It is a reminder of the pain of being an outsider; of the sustaining power of love and the destructive power of hate; and of the human will to triumph.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/2408.Ian_McEwan">About Ian McEwan</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/2408.Ian_McEwan"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1404240951p3/2408.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/2408.Ian_McEwan">Ian McEwan
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
12,193
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5460292905736268649">Ian McEwan studied at the University of Sussex, where he received a BA degree in English Literature in 1970 and later received his MA degree in English Literature at the University of East Anglia.<br/><br/>McEwan's works have earned him worldwide critical acclaim. He won the Somerset Maugham Award in 1976 for his first collection of short stories <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3526.First_Love__Last_Rites' title='First Love, Last Rites' rel='nofollow noopener'>First Love, Last Rites</a>; the Whitbread Novel Award (1987) and </span>
  <span id="freeText5460292905736268649" style="display:none">Ian McEwan studied at the University of Sussex, where he received a BA degree in English Literature in 1970 and later received his MA degree in English Literature at the University of East Anglia.<br /><br />McEwan's works have earned him worldwide critical acclaim. He won the Somerset Maugham Award in 1976 for his first collection of short stories <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3526.First_Love__Last_Rites" title="First Love, Last Rites" rel="nofollow noopener">First Love, Last Rites</a>; the Whitbread Novel Award (1987) and the Prix Fémina Etranger (1993) for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6869.The_Child_in_Time" title="The Child in Time" rel="nofollow noopener">The Child in Time</a>; and Germany's Shakespeare Prize in 1999. He has been shortlisted for the Man Booker Prize for Fiction numerous times, winning the award for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6862.Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam" rel="nofollow noopener">Amsterdam</a> in 1998. His novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6867.Atonement" title="Atonement" rel="nofollow noopener">Atonement</a> received the WH Smith Literary Award (2002), National Book Critics' Circle Fiction Award (2003), Los Angeles Times Prize for Fiction (2003), and the Santiago Prize for the European Novel (2004). He was awarded a CBE in 2000. In 2006, he won the James Tait Black Memorial Prize for his novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5015.Saturday" title="Saturday" rel="nofollow noopener">Saturday</a> and his novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/815309.On_Chesil_Beach" title="On Chesil Beach" rel="nofollow noopener">On Chesil Beach</a> was named Galaxy Book of the Year at the 2008 British Book Awards where McEwan was also named Reader's Digest Author of the Year.<br /><br />McEwan lives in London.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5460292905736268649" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/50111227-nutshell">Quotes from Nutshell</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;A strange mood has seized the almost-educated young. They're on the march, angry at times, but mostly needful, longing for authority's blessing, its validation of their chosen <i>identities</i>. The decline of the West in new guise perhaps. Or the exaltation and liberation of the self. A social-media site famously proposes seventy-one gender options – neutrois, two spirit, bigender…any colour you like, Mr Ford. Biology is not destiny after all, and there's cause for celebration. A shrimp is neither limiting nor stable. I declare my undeniable feeling for who I am. If I turn out to be white, I may identify as black. And vice versa. I may announce myself as disabled, or disabled in context. If my identity is that of a believer, I'm easily wounded, my flesh torn to bleeding by any questioning of my faith. Offended, I enter a state of grace. Should inconvenient opinions hover near me like fallen angels or evil djinn (a mile being too near), I'll be in need of the special campus safe room equipped with Play-Doh and looped footage of gambolling puppies. Ah, the intellectual life! I may need advance warning if upsetting books or ideas threaten my very being by coming too close, breathing on my face, my brain, like unwholesome drugs.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/7910552-a-strange-mood-has-seized-the-almost-educated-young-they-re-on">36 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;It's already clear to me how much of life is forgotten even as it happens. Most of it. The unregarded present spooling away from us, the soft tumble of unremarkable thoughts, the long-neglected miracle of existence.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/7950261-it-s-already-clear-to-me-how-much-of-life-is">33 likes</a>
            </nobr>
